    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7801853759406760840">The Insulted & Injured, published soon after Dostoevsky's political imprisonment, clearly foreshadows his later preoccupation with unconscious psychological drives & their external effects on the lives of his characters. Where his later works carry these drives to inevitably dramatic conclusions, The Insulted & Injured confines them within the smaller boundaries of everyda</span>
  <span id="freeText7801853759406760840" style="display:none">The Insulted &amp; Injured, published soon after Dostoevsky's political imprisonment, clearly foreshadows his later preoccupation with unconscious psychological drives &amp; their external effects on the lives of his characters. Where his later works carry these drives to inevitably dramatic conclusions, The Insulted &amp; Injured confines them within the smaller boundaries of everyday event.<br /> In this story the impulse toward self-abnegation in love, which appears so markedly in both Vanya &amp; Natasha, isn't itself enough to direct their lives; instead, it combines with their social world &amp; the mundane ambitions of Prince Valkovsky to defeat their hope of happiness. Of all the characters in the novel, only Natasha's lover, the Prince's son Alyosha-the person least driven to mold life to his own terms-emerges untouched.<br /> Here are, to a greater extent than in Dostoevsky's more familiar works, flesh-&amp;-blood people as we see them around every day. They are made up of both good &amp; evil, of will &amp; acceptance. Unfailingly they command interest &amp; illuminate understanding.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7801853759406760840" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer584491103" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10350972597050746722">Unizhennye i oskorblennye = Humiliés et offensés = The Insulted and Humiliated, Fyodor Dostoyevsky <br/><br/>Natasha leaves her parents' home and runs away with Alyosha (prince Alexey) – the son of Prince Valkovsky. As a result of his pain, her father, Nikolai, curses her. The only friend that remains by Natasha's side is Ivan – her childhood friend who is deeply in love with her, and whom Natasha has rejected despite their being engaged. Prince Valkovsky tries to destroy Alyosha's plans to marry Natasha,</span>
  <span id="freeText10350972597050746722" style="display:none">Unizhennye i oskorblennye = Humiliés et offensés = The Insulted and Humiliated, Fyodor Dostoyevsky <br /><br />Natasha leaves her parents' home and runs away with Alyosha (prince Alexey) – the son of Prince Valkovsky. As a result of his pain, her father, Nikolai, curses her. The only friend that remains by Natasha's side is Ivan – her childhood friend who is deeply in love with her, and whom Natasha has rejected despite their being engaged. Prince Valkovsky tries to destroy Alyosha's plans to marry Natasha, and wants to make him marry the rich princess Katerina. Alyosha is a naïve but loveable young man who is easily manipulated by his father. Following his father's plan, Alyosha falls in love with Katerina, but still loves Natasha. He is constantly torn between these two women, too indecisive and infatuated with both to make a decision. Eventually, Natasha sacrifices her own feelings and withdraws in order for Alyosha to choose Katerina. Meanwhile, Ivan rescues an orphan girl, Elena (known as Nellie), from the clutches of a procuress and learns that her mother ran away from her father's (Jeremy Smith's) home with her sweetheart, a man who abandoned her when Nellie's mother gave birth. It is later revealed that Prince Valkovsky is Nellie's father. Her parents were legally married, but Prince Valkovsky persuaded his young and innocent wife to rob her father, Jeremy. After moving to Petersburg, Nellie's mother asks forgiveness to her father, but he rejects them. Before dying, Nellie's mother makes Nellie promise to never go to her real father, whose name is on a document she leaves her daughter. In attempt to make Nikolai (Natasha's father) reconcile with Natasha, Ivan persuades Nikolai and his wife to adopt Nellie. By telling them her life story, Nellie makes Nikolai's heart soften and he forgives Natasha and removes his curse, and they are reunited. Natasha's family plans to move from Petersburg, but just before they leave Nellie dies from a chronic heart condition; the little girl makes it clear to Ivan she does not forgive her father for his cruel treatment of her mother. She also tells him he should marry Natasha. The story ends on an ambiguous note with Natasha and Ivan reflecting on the events that have unfolded.<br /><br /><br />عنوانها: آزردگان؛ نفرین شدگان؛ تحقیر و توهین شده ها؛ رنج کشیدگان و خوارشدگان؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و سوم ماه آوریل سال 1972 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: آزردگان؛ نویسنده: فئودور داستایوسکی؛ مترجم: ربیع مشفق همدانی؛ تهران، صفیعلیشاه، 1327؛ در 466 ص؛ چاپ یازدهم 1359؛ چاپ سیزدهم 1362؛ چاپ دیگر: در 517 ص، صفیعلیشاه، 1388؛ شابک: 9789645626646؛ <br />عنوان: آزردگان؛ نویسنده: فئودور داستایوسکی؛ مترجم: بهزاد؛ بی جا، شهریار، 1327؛ در 575 ص؛ <br />عنوان: آزردگان؛ نویسنده: فئودور داستایوسکی؛ مترجم: عنایت الله شکیباپور؛ تهران، ؟، ؟؛ در 575 ص؛ چاپ دیگر: تهران، دنیای کتاب، 1388؛ در 575 ص؛ شابک: 9643460495؛ <br />عنوان: آزردگان؛ نویسنده: فئودور داستایوسکی؛ مترجم: فهیمه حصارکی؛ تهران، نگارستان کتاب، 1389؛ در 620 ص؛ شابک: 9786001900198؛<br />عنوان: نفرین شدگان؛ نویسنده: فئودور داستایوسکی؛ مترجم: اسماعیل قهرمانی پور؛ تهران، سمیر، 1393؛ در 464 ص؛ شابک: 9789642201471؛<br />عنوان: تحقیر و توهین شده ها؛ نویسنده: فئودور داستایوسکی؛ مترجم: پرویز شهدی؛ تهران، به سخن : مجید، 1394؛ در 504 ص؛ شابک: 9786009484447؛<br />عنوان: رنج کشیدگان و خوارشدگان؛ نویسنده: فئودور داستایوسکی؛ مترجم: محسن کرمی؛ تهران، نیلوفر، 1394؛ در 480 ص؛ <br /><br />کتاب نخستین بار در سال 1861 میلادی منتشر شد. اثر بزرگ داستایوسکی ست که ایشان پس از بازگشت از سیبری بنوشتند. قهرمان رمان،‌ وانیا، سرگذشت خویش می‌گوید. بهتر است کتاب را بارها خود بخوانید، هرچند میدانم مناسب این روزهای پر از دیدنیهای تصویری و وبگاههای روزآمد نیست؛ نخست خلاصه داستان را بنوشته بودم؛ اما بیشتر دوستان نازنین احساس خویش مینویسند که چرا خلاصه داستان را بنوشته ام، پس از نگارش همه را دوباره پاک کردم. شخصیتها: وانیا راوی؛ ناتاشا دختر عاشق؛ آلیوشا پسر شاهزاده؛ اخمنینف پدر ناتاشا؛ شاهزاده والکوسکی پدر آلیوشا؛ و اسمیت. ا. شربیانی</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1812890680378584027">Why is this one not as 'famous' as Crime and Punishment or The Brothers Karamazov? It may not be as deep philosophically as either of those, but trust me, it aches your heart all the more for it. <i>Humiliated and Insulted</i> turned my insides into a weepy mess. Characters in a book, or real people? My mind and heart could not tell, or maybe they could, but it did not make a difference. Dostoevsky is that good, and I can only thank him for this beautiful novel. </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer410018735" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11230158439841260757">OH! What a novel! How is it possible a mere <i>human</i> could write something so wonderful and compelling? <i>The Insulted and Injured</i> is a beautiful, painful novel. It is not as complicated as <i>War and Peace</i>. The plot is not as philisophical as <i>The Brother's Karamazov </i>. It is not as well as known as <i>Crime and Punishment</i>. And <i>yet</i>...and yet it deserves a ranking alongside its more classic and well-loved brethren. The themes may be simple, but the message rings true. <br/><br/><b> Plot</b><br/>Vanya is a young writer, initially </span>
  <span id="freeText11230158439841260757" style="display:none">OH! What a novel! How is it possible a mere <i>human</i> could write something so wonderful and compelling? <i>The Insulted and Injured</i> is a beautiful, painful novel. It is not as complicated as <i>War and Peace</i>. The plot is not as philisophical as <i>The Brother's Karamazov </i>. It is not as well as known as <i>Crime and Punishment</i>. And <i>yet</i>...and yet it deserves a ranking alongside its more classic and well-loved brethren. The themes may be simple, but the message rings true. <br /><br /><b> Plot</b><br />Vanya is a young writer, initially successful and in love with the lovely Natasha. Natasha, though, falls for a different young man, Alyosha, and runs away with him. They are not married. Opposed by Natasha's parents, who have cut her off, and Alyosha's father, the two turn to Vanya as their only friend. Plauged by illness, despair, poverty, Vanya navigates the waters of love and forgiveness to care for the woman he loves and the man she chose. <br /><i>The Insulted and Injured</i> explores forigveness and love, innocence and youth, passion and family. <br /><br /><b>Thoughts</b><br />The ending came to soon. <br />Eagerly, eagerly I read. I wanted to know what would happen. I <i>needed</i> to know. And now I know and I wish it hadn't ended. I could have gone on reading for days. The characters that reach out and tug on your heart, the passion of family and friends, the selfless and selfishness found on each page. Dostoyevsky, you are a master. I can say no more. This book is amazong. Wonderful. It comes with my highest reccomendation. I don't want to ever recover from the spell. <br />I can only imagine how much <i>better</i> this book must be in Russian.<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2920939711" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2920939711" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2920939711?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781592247417" href="/book/show/2191100.The_Insulted_and_Injured">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2920939711" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer296942165549420104"><u>The Insulted and the Injured</u> goes also by the titles <u>The Insulted and the Humiliated</u> and <u>Injury and Insult</u>. It was first published in 1861 in the monthly magazine <i>Vremya</i>. It was Dostoyevsky’s first novel published after returning from his deportation to Siberia. The translation I read is by Constance Garner. I wonder how it compares to Ignat Avsey’s. <br/><br/><u>
  <b>Primary Characters</b>
</u>:<br/>*Vanya (Ivan Petrovich) – author and narrator<br/>*Nikolai Sergueych Ikhmenev – landlord of Ikhmenevka<br/>*Anna Andreyevna – Nikolai'</span>
  <span id="freeText296942165549420104" style="display:none"><u>The Insulted and the Injured</u> goes also by the titles <u>The Insulted and the Humiliated</u> and <u>Injury and Insult</u>. It was first published in 1861 in the monthly magazine <i>Vremya</i>. It was Dostoyevsky’s first novel published after returning from his deportation to Siberia. The translation I read is by Constance Garner. I wonder how it compares to Ignat Avsey’s. <br /><br /><u>
  <b>Primary Characters</b>
</u>:<br />*Vanya (Ivan Petrovich) – author and narrator<br />*Nikolai Sergueych Ikhmenev – landlord of Ikhmenevka<br />*Anna Andreyevna – Nikolai's wife<br />*Natasha (Natalya Nikolayevna) – Nikolai's daughter<br />*Mavra – Natasha's maid<br />*Prince Valkovsky – no spoiler is given here!<br />*Alyosha (Prince Alexey) – Prince Valkovsky's son<br />*Katya (Katerina Fiodorovna) – heiress <br />*Jeremy Smith<br />*Nellie (Elena) – a 13-year-old orphan, Jeremy Smith's granddaughter<br />*Filipp Filippych Masloboyev – Vanya's old friend<br />*Alexandra Semionovna – Filipp's wife<br /><br />The story is told by a narrator, an author who has recently published his first book. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a> had recently published <u>
  <b>his</b>
</u> own first novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67326.Poor_Folk" title="Poor Folk by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Poor Folk</a>. The narrator (Vanya) is telling a story about himself, a girl he once loved (Natasha) and a young girl (Nellie) who strangely crosses his path. He was to marry Natasha, until that fateful day she fell head over heels in love with Alyosha. The story becomes a tad more complicated; Alyosha’s father wants his son to marry a wealthy heiress. There are two plot lines—Nellie’s and Vanya’s. By the end, Dostoyevsky has cleverly intertwined the two. From the start, with the death of a man and his beloved dog, the reader’s interest is piqued. <br /><br />From the above one cannot determine if the story will pull you in. To determine that, one must look at <b>
  <u>how</u>
</b> the story is told. It focuses on character portrayal. The characters have widely differing personalities. Even the most malevolent of them all is <b>
  <u>not</u>
</b> a cardboard figure. I am here speaking of Prince Valkovsky. Every bit you learn about each character pulls the reader in and makes you want to learn more. It is through one’s interest in the characters that the reader then wants to know how the story will end. Who will end up with whom? Who will be forgiven and who will not? Which relationships will be reconciled? Which will not?<br /><br /> More questions arise. How does one define genuine love? Does love demand sacrifice? Is love explainable? The child Nellie´s intuitive ability to sense who can be trusted and who cannot is an ability which I think many children have. Complicated thought analyses do not get in the way as they so often do for an adult. Dostoyevsky’s knowledge and understanding of how people behave lies at the base of this novel. It is this that makes it so special. <br /><br />One cannot help but think about how it comes to be that people are so very different. You think about this because Dostoyevsky draws each so realistically. There <u>
  <b>are</b>
</u> people like Prince Valkovsky! There <b>
  <u>are</u>
</b> those like his son Alyosha—they charm you, they are easy to like even though you know they are weak, evasive and constantly prevaricate. In this story, I particularly like Dostoyevsky’s female characters. Natasha is straightforward and clear thinking. She is the one, who when discussions derail, poses a simple question that brings the discussion back on track. I have spoken of Nellie earlier. Basically, what I am saying is that each character comes alive. Each has an identity that pushes you to relate to them and to compare them to people your yourself know. <br /><br />The ending, it too is very good. Neither too happy, nor too sad. Just enough information is given so that readers can draw their own conclusion on how events will play out in the future. <br /><br />Now look at the titles. They point out another central question posed. How is suffering to be redressed? Are insults, humiliation and inflicted injuries to be forgiven? Can and should all be forgiven? And now read this quote:<br /><br />“…she seemed purposely trying to aggravate her wound by this mysterious behavior, this mistrustfulness of us all, as though she enjoyed her own pain by this egoism of suffering, if I may so express it. This aggravation of suffering and this reveling in it, I could understand. It is the enjoyment of man, of the insulted and injured, depressed by destiny and smarting under the sense of its injustice.”<br /><br />Here we see how Dostoyevsky has pushed the discussion of man’s attitude toward suffering deeper, by pointing out that we often revel in our own pain. Do we draw it out to feel sorry for ourself? Are we searching for pity or do we do this to get solace from loved ones? It is an interesting question. <br /><br />Dostoyevsky always gets me thinking. His characters are complicated; they are real. It is for these two reasons I so very much enjoy reading his books. <br /><br />I don't like at all the audiobook narration by Alastair Cameron. It is over-dramatized, often too quickly read and his female intonations annoy me immensely. His male intonations are somewhat better but here the pacing is off. It does not sound natural; the reading is jerky and stilted. The wrong words are emphasized. I do not recommend listening to this audiobook. Listening was a chore. The narration by Cameron I have given one star. <br /><br />It is crazy that many have not heard of this novel! I had passed it up earlier thinking it was a short story. What a terrible mistake.<br /><br /><br />*******************************<br /><br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7144.Crime_and_Punishment" title="Crime and Punishment by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Crime and Punishment</a> 5 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18522230.The_Idiot" title="The Idiot by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Idiot</a> 5 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2191100.The_Insulted_and_Injured" title="The Insulted and Injured by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Insulted and Injured</a> 4 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17877.The_House_of_the_Dead" title="The House of the Dead by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The House of the Dead</a> 3 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17690219.The_Brothers_Karamazov" title="The Brothers Karamazov by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Brothers <div id="review_421858146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=borscht-and-kvass">borscht-and-kvass</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=pre-1900s">pre-1900s</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer421858146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16202688790963885936">A bracing early novel from the most unflinching of the Russian Titans, <i>(The) Humiliated & Insulted</i> is the only Dostoevsky novel with a writer-as-narrator, but not the only based on autobiographical material. Vanya is an up-and-coming literary talent whose first novel—cough not <i>Poor Folk</i> cough—has been critically lauded. He is chummy with Natasha, who is overly chummy with the indecisive blithering imbecile Alyosha, who can’t choose between marrying Nat or a less-attractive Countess with a large </span>
  <span id="freeText16202688790963885936" style="display:none">A bracing early novel from the most unflinching of the Russian Titans, <i>(The) Humiliated &amp; Insulted</i> is the only Dostoevsky novel with a writer-as-narrator, but not the only based on autobiographical material. Vanya is an up-and-coming literary talent whose first novel—cough not <i>Poor Folk</i> cough—has been critically lauded. He is chummy with Natasha, who is overly chummy with the indecisive blithering imbecile Alyosha, who can’t choose between marrying Nat or a less-attractive Countess with a large fortune. His father, the Prince Valkovsky, the villain of the novel, wants to get his seedy mitts on the money to spend on debauchery and immorality of disgraceful proportions (although nothing you wouldn’t see outside an Essex pub on a Friday night). Toss into this the mercurial orphan Nelly who the narrator takes pity on, and you have an extremely gripping and wrenching novel from a writer who you always forget is so damned <i>entertaining</i>. Ignat Avsey’s controversial translation of <i>The Brothers Karamazov</i> (which he re-titled to <i>The Karamazov Brothers</i>) was not entirely lauded for its sympathy to the Russian original. But this is a very readable, decent translation, albeit with the odd Anglicism and Americanism creeping in here and there. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16202688790963885936" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText2609853730888320096" style="display:none">It has become one of my favorites from Dostoevsky. The book is focused on social classes as well as the human soul. Characters, dialogues, emotional conflicts, all those elements make the book a whole package and it is one of the most flowing novels of the writer. I also like Brothers Karamazhov but that wasn't as flowing as this book. Especially the parts about cardinals and monastery at Karamazhovs were hard to read for me. <br /><br />The Insulted and Humiliated is humanistic, psychological and thought-provoking and I don't think I am exaggerating by saying these. The woman characters are especially remarkable at the book and that is another reason to love the book for me.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/36492045-the-insulted-and-humiliated">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18940246-sotiris-karaiskos?shelf=russian-literature">russian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18940246-sotiris-karaiskos?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1010110190" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14438003703700037385">After many fluctuations, experiments, searches, and of course many hardships, the author has finally reached a state where, having found his own voice, he was able to write his immortal masterpieces. Of course for the specialists and for many non-specialists this book is clearly inferior to his most well-known works and I'm largely bound to agree but this cannot change my perception that it is a great book. The elements that make his best works stand out are all here, perhaps in their freer but </span>
  <span id="freeText14438003703700037385" style="display:none">After many fluctuations, experiments, searches, and of course many hardships, the author has finally reached a state where, having found his own voice, he was able to write his immortal masterpieces. Of course for the specialists and for many non-specialists this book is clearly inferior to his most well-known works and I'm largely bound to agree but this cannot change my perception that it is a great book. The elements that make his best works stand out are all here, perhaps in their freer but more raw form, with the profound psychological analysis of characters that somehow lead to social analysis, with the emotional overflowing in a way which reinforces the dynamic of the text, with a profound humanism and clear perceptions in favor of ordinary people, and obviously many more that I imagine there are others more competent to analyze.<br /><br />This very moving story is divided into two parts that are joined by the narrator's voice until they are finally connected. In the first, a woman falls in love but this love becomes her suffering because of the naivety and indecision of her beloved, the second one concerns a 13 year old girl who carries a tragic story and seeks only affection and kindness. Their common characteristic is the suffering, but in a sense that escapes the usual pattern of misfortune as our heroines, each for their own reasons, choose to suffer even more, believing that they deserve this, as a kind of remorse, drawing, in the end, a strange form of contentment, and so the author finds a reason to expand his thinking on this characteristic of human nature as in addition to these two we find corresponding cases who are tormented by their pride that does not stand insults. In the latter, the amoral aristocrat arrives who enjoys the power he has over others, and his cynical confession is one of the most intense parts of the book, revealing the author's fear of a new world coming where such personalities dominate. In the end, only the moral power of the people can provide a solution and the physical goodness of the people can confront anything evil.<br /><br />All this in a very moving novel in which the writer makes us in his own unique way to become participants in the suffering of our heroes and to experience all the difficulties of their lives, with the constant humiliating situations that lead to particular psychological processes that we can understand as they are familiar to us. His writing has such an intensity, such a dramatic power that it can overwhelm the reader and move him to such an extent that an emotional burden is created that makes the reading itself a redemptive torture that he will gladly accept. But beyond that, the author's way of conveying his own opinions and perceptions so effortlessly and his own humanitarian message adds much more and this overall feeling at the end providing the certainty that there is something very important in these pages. which were followed, of course, by even more important works in the rest of this marvelous writing career.<br /><br />Μετά από πολλές διακυμάνσεις, πειραματισμούς, αναζητήσεις και φυσικά πολλές κακουχίες ο συγγραφέας έφτασε επιτέλους σε μία κατάσταση που έχοντας βρει τη δική του φωνή ήταν σε θέση να γράψει τα αθάνατα αριστουργήματα του. Βέβαια για τους ειδικούς και για πολλούς λιγότερο ειδικούς αυτό το βιβλίο είναι σαφέστατα υποδεέστερο από τα πιο γνωστά του έργα και σε μεγάλο βαθμό είναι αναγκασμένος να συμφωνήσω αλλά αυτό δεν μπορεί να αλλάξει την αντίληψή μου ότι πρόκειται για ένα σπουδαίο βιβλίο. Τα στοιχεία που κάνουν το έργο του να ξεχωρίζει υπάρχουν όλα εδώ, ίσως στην πιο ελεύθερη αλλά και πιο ακατέργαστη μορφή τους, με την βαθύτατη ψυχολογική ανάλυση των χαρακτήρων που οδηγούν με κάποιον τρόπο σε μία κοινωνική ανάλυση, με το συναίσθημα να ξεχειλίζει αλλά με έναν τρόπο που ενισχύει τη δυναμική του κειμένου, με έναν βαθύτατο ανθρωπισμό και ξεκάθαρες αντιλήψεις υπέρ των απλών ανθρώπων και προφανώς πολλά άλλα που φαντάζομαι ότι υπάρχουν περισσότερο αρμόδιοι να τα αναλύσουν.<br /><br />Αυτή η πολύ συγκινητική ιστορία χωρίζεται σε δύο σκέλη που τα ενώνει η φωνή του αφηγητή μέχρι που στο τέλος συνδέονται. Στο πρώτο μία γυναίκα ερωτεύεται αλλά αυτός ο έρωτας γίνεται το βάσανο της εξαιτίας της αφέλειας και της αναποφασιστικότητας του αγαπημένου της, το δεύτερο αφορά ένα κορίτσι 13 ετών που κουβαλάει μία τραγική ιστορία και αναζητάει μόνο στοργή και καλοσύνη. Κοινό χαρακτηριστικό τους είναι τα βάσανα αλλά με μία έννοια που ξεφεύγει από το συνηθισμένο μοτίβο της κακοτυχίας καθώς οι ηρωίδες μας, η καθεμία για δικούς της λόγους, επιλέγουν να βασανίζονται ακόμα περισσότερο, πιστεύοντας ότι κάτι τέτοιο αξίζουν, σαν ένα είδος μεταμέλειας, αντλώντας στο τέλος μία παράξενη μορφή ικανοποίησης και κάπως έτσι ο συγγραφέας βρίσκει τη δική του αφορμή για να επεκτείνει τη σκέψη του πάνω σε αυτό το χαρακτηριστικό της ανθρώπινης φύσης καθώς εκτός από αυτές τις δύο συναντάμε και αντίστοιχες περιπτώσεις ανθρώπων που βασανίζονται εξαιτίας της περηφάνιας τους που δεν σηκώνει προσβολές. Σε αυτό το τελευταίο κάνει την εμφάνισή του ο αμοραλιστής αριστοκράτης που απολαμβάνει την δύναμη που έχει πάνω στους άλλους και η κυνική ομολογία του είναι ένα από τα πιο έντονα μέρη του βιβλίου και αποκαλύπτει το φόβο του συγγραφέα για ένα νέο κόσμο μου έρχεται όπου κυριαρχούν τέτοιες μορφές, από τις οποίες πηγάζει οτιδήποτε σάπιο. Στο τέλος μόνο η ηθική δύναμη των ανθρώπων μπορεί να δώσει κάποια λύση και η φυσική καλοσύνη των ανθρώπων να αναμετρηθεί με το κακό.<br /><br />Όλα αυτά σε ένα ιδιαίτερα συγκινητικό μυθιστόρημα στο όποιο ο συγγραφέας μας κάνει με το δικό του μοναδικό τρόπο να γινόμαστε συμμέτοχοι των βασάνων των ηρώων μας και να νιώθουμε όλες τις δυσκολίες της ζωής τους, με τις συνεχόμενες ταπεινωτικές καταστάσεις που οδηγούν σε ιδιαίτερες ψυχολογικές διεργασίες με τις οποίες μπορούμε να συμπάσχουμε καθώς πάντα μας είναι οικείες. Ο τρόπος γραφής του έχει μία τέτοια ένταση, μία τέτοια δραματική δύναμη που μπορεί να πλημμυρίσει τον αναγνώστη και να τον συγκινήσει σε τέτοιο βαθμό που να του δημιουργήσει ένα συναισθηματικό βάρος που κάνει την ανάγνωση να είναι από μόνη της ένα λυτρωτικό βασανιστήριο που με ευχαρίστηση θα το αποδεχτούμε. Αλλά και πέρα από αυτό ο τρόπος του συγγραφέα να μας μεταφέρει τόσο αβίαστα τις δικές του απόψεις και αντιλήψεις και το δικό του ανθρωπιστικό μήνυμα προσθέτει πολλά περισσότερα και αυτή η συνολική αίσθηση είναι στο τέλος που προσφέρει τη βεβαιότητα ότι υπάρχει κάτι πολύ σπουδαίο σε αυτές τις σελίδες που ακολουθήθηκε φυσικά από ακόμα σπουδαιότερα έργα στη συνέχεια αυτής της θαυμαστής συγγραφικής πορείας.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14438003703700037385" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer15143903041359734146">This book would have only gotten 3 stars if it weren't for its final page, which was awesome and changed the book for me entirely. I started off really enjoying the read and the dramatic style of 19th C writers, but I tired of it by the end. I kept thinking of Dickens and all the other 19th C writers who left no plot line hanging and no character, however minor, without an end, or a solution, even if it consisted of a brief sentence in parentheses explaining what had come of so and so. Dostoevsk</span>
  <span id="freeText15143903041359734146" style="display:none">This book would have only gotten 3 stars if it weren't for its final page, which was awesome and changed the book for me entirely. I started off really enjoying the read and the dramatic style of 19th C writers, but I tired of it by the end. I kept thinking of Dickens and all the other 19th C writers who left no plot line hanging and no character, however minor, without an end, or a solution, even if it consisted of a brief sentence in parentheses explaining what had come of so and so. Dostoevsky also gives the most perfect characterization of a sociopath I have ever read. If you want to know what a sociopath is, skip <i>The Sociopath Next Door</i>, which was lame, and read this instead.</span>

<div id="review_839211644" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/839211644" />
    <a title="Beatrice Apetrei" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22548668-beatrice-apetrei"><img alt="Beatrice Apetrei" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586449703p2/22548668.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/839211644?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beatrice Apetrei" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beatrice Apetrei" href="/user/show/22548668-beatrice-apetrei">Beatrice Apetrei</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548668-beatrice-apetrei?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548668-beatrice-apetrei?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548668-beatrice-apetrei?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548668-beatrice-apetrei?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer839211644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9542659474855999631">So far, I’ve only read 4 novels written by Mr. Dostoyevsky, the fourth being The Insulted and Humiliated ( the others : The Eternal Husband, Notes from Underground and The Adolescent). The first three books written by him that I’ve read made me feel a lot of sadness, regret, helplessness, and so every time I felt like picking up a book written by him, I decided not to. I was scared of having my <i> good</i> mood ruined or of being induced into a certain mood that I could not stand. <br/>A few weeks ago I de</span>
  <span id="freeText9542659474855999631" style="display:none">So far, I’ve only read 4 novels written by Mr. Dostoyevsky, the fourth being The Insulted and Humiliated ( the others : The Eternal Husband, Notes from Underground and The Adolescent). The first three books written by him that I’ve read made me feel a lot of sadness, regret, helplessness, and so every time I felt like picking up a book written by him, I decided not to. I was scared of having my <i> good</i> mood ruined or of being induced into a certain mood that I could not stand. <br />A few weeks ago I decided that I wouldn’t care. I missed his exceptional <b> writing</b>, his <i>honesty</i>, his attention to detail, his <i>caring</i> for others and for the world. I missed his first view perspective and how centered on human feelings he was. I needed something of the kind. <br />I have to admit that I still carried, in the depths of my mind, the thought that he might be as helpless as in his other works (that I’ve read, of course; by helpless, I actually mean his protagonists. It’s a feeling that I was left with after reading the other three mentioned novels). And I was having a great week. But I tried. And no regrets followed.<br /><br /><i>The Insulted and Humiliated</i> was such an extraordinary experience for me. <br /><br />The action takes place in Dostoyevsky’s beloved Petersburg, in the 19th century. If you’ve read any of his novels, you already know this famous city, the cold and misty nights, the wind; the paved alleys, the shadows, the old houses. There is a series of images pictured in most of his novels, images that create the <i>appropriate</i> background for what is to be told.<br /><br /><b>The characters and plot.</b> <br />We are introduced to Vanya, a poor writer, who is narrating the events, giving us an insight of his feelings and thoughts concerning the development of the plot. He is mainly an observer, but also the one both Natasha and Alyosha turn to in times of need. Vanya loves Natasha and once intended to marry her, but the circumstances made it so Natasha fell in love with young and childish Alyosha, who, for well-known reasons, won’t marry her. This fact humiliates and saddens both Natasha and her father, who, in his anger, decides to slowly let go of all the beautiful memories of his daughter as if she were dead.<br />This is a journey of learning to understand, accept and forgive. Vanya needs to selflessly love and accept Alyosha as a friend disregarding his jealousy of him. Alyosha must learn to face his father and grow up into the man everyone expects him to be. Natasha has to face reality and let go of what she is not meant to have. But how much of what is asked of them, can they provide? <br /><br /><b>The writing. </b>The narration is mainly composed of dialogue through which we are introduced to everyone’s actions, wishes, last events, feelings. This way of presenting the action, in my opinion, gives more insight into how the characters are and their motifs (the way they talk, what are they focused on, the rhythm etc.); and so they are given more dimension. The characters are so horrifyingly real that you can’t really know if the presented content took birth in Dostoyevsky’s mind, or if it was inspired by real events that the author took part in. <br /><br />I have to add that I personally loved Natasha. She is mature, strong, faithful, but still sensitive and easily broken. She loves, forgives, appreciates. And what I found to be the most precious feature of hers, was her <i>selfless</i> love for Alyosha. A love void of selfishness. She loved him for him, and not for what he was giving her, and that is a quality and a power so hardly found in humans. <br /><br />If I recommend this book? Certainly yes. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9542659474855999631" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1969102477" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1969102477" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1895570-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484874117p2/1895570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1969102477?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/30639413-the-insulted-and-the-injured">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=dostoyevsky">dostoyevsky</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1969102477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12052397218815378900">Sometimes even a minor work by a great author can endear one. In this case, it is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30639413.The_Insulted_and_the_Injured' title='The Insulted and the Injured by Fyodor Dostoyevsky' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Insulted and the Injured</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky' title='Fyodor Dostoyevsky' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>. While it is not the first book he wrote after returning to Petersburg from his stint in Siberia -- both <b>Uncle's Dream</b> and <b>The Village of Stepanchikovo</b> were published earlier -- it is the first real hint of the greatness that was to come.<br/><br/>The story is about a young author (suspiciously like Dostoyevsky) named Ivan Petrovich who falls in love with a young woman who c</span>
  <span id="freeText12052397218815378900" style="display:none">Sometimes even a minor work by a great author can endear one. In this case, it is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30639413.The_Insulted_and_the_Injured" title="The Insulted and the Injured by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Insulted and the Injured</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>. While it is not the first book he wrote after returning to Petersburg from his stint in Siberia -- both <b>Uncle's Dream</b> and <b>The Village of Stepanchikovo</b> were published earlier -- it is the first real hint of the greatness that was to come.<br /><br />The story is about a young author (suspiciously like Dostoyevsky) named Ivan Petrovich who falls in love with a young woman who chooses instead to run off with a feckless young nobleman. He remains involved with the young woman and her parents, and soon also with a 14-year-old gamine who is suffering from consumption. This girl, Nellie, is brilliantly characterized without sentimentality. Also notable is the evil Prince Valkovsky, who tries to manipulate all the other characters in the novel.<br /><br /><b>The Insulted and Injured</b> is not frequently read, perhaps because it starts so slowly. But once little Nellie is introduced, the tale's pace becomes as feverish as she is. It is worth following Dostoyevsky's footsteps, because I am continuing to find treasures among his minor works.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12052397218815378900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2501400514" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15181254352844341684">In a culture of busy-ness that bleeds into the way we read and enjoy books, the slow and steady burn of Dostoyevsky’s writing does the soul good. With rich writing and proper character development, The Insulted and Injured displays the glories of love and heartbreak, of pride and forgiveness, of selfishness and selflessness. The feeling of accomplishment and increased intelligence is an added bonus of reading Russian literature!</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_265629948" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265629948" />
    <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond"><img alt="Bryn Hammond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523356367p2/7224461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/265629948?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryn Hammond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryn Hammond" href="/user/show/7224461-bryn-hammond">Bryn Hammond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802825902" href="/book/show/11595895-the-insulted-and-injured">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7224461-bryn-hammond?shelf=novelists-or-shorts">novelists-or-shorts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265629948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer304988782232212858">A writer at the time said the book was ‘beneath aesthetic criticism; but he acknowledged everyone had been reading what stood out as the most interesting Russian novel published in 1861.’ [Joseph Frank, <i>Dostoevsky: The Stir of Liberation, 1860-1865</i>] Funny, that’s how I felt: it’s awful, but it’s interesting. <br/><br/>It seems to be his most derivative work yet (English Dickens and the French ‘social-humanitarian’ school). D.’s obsessive urge to describe not only ‘the insulted and injured’ but the functio</span>
  <span id="freeText304988782232212858" style="display:none">A writer at the time said the book was ‘beneath aesthetic criticism; but he acknowledged everyone had been reading what stood out as the most interesting Russian novel published in 1861.’ [Joseph Frank, <i>Dostoevsky: The Stir of Liberation, 1860-1865</i>] Funny, that’s how I felt: it’s awful, but it’s interesting. <br /><br />It seems to be his most derivative work yet (English Dickens and the French ‘social-humanitarian’ school). D.’s obsessive urge to describe not only ‘the insulted and injured’ but the functions of the ego whereby insults are clung to and injuries self-inflicted stands out to disadvantage against trite plot turns. Way overwritten; then again the ‘I’ is a novelist in D.’s own situation, who accepts he’s a hack and writes in furious haste because he has to eat. <br /><br />But I don’t know where he gets his material. From the French? Not from the English. Or from life? Child prostitution. Sexual exploitation of other kinds. Women who live with men unmarried, their conditions and their fates. These are certainly more politely touched upon in the English novel, as far as I’m aware; I’ve forgotten what’s in Eugene Sue’s <i>The Mysteries of Paris</i> (which I did read in its Dedalus abridgement) but perhaps the French social-humanitarians went this far with realism in their melodramas. At least I remember Fantine’s story in <i>Les Mis</i>, so I’ll note against her example: these fallen woman are in no way blamed by the author’s presentation; although one of D.’s women has as sad an end as Fantine, another achieves a happy reconciliation – on her terms – with her family. While a third woman is not even abandoned by her lover, but we are asked to smile at their domestic scenes – spats and idiosyncrasies and understated love. <br /><br />I lapped this book up in my student days, and embarrassingly I can see why. Dostoyevsky makes great reading for students.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="304988782232212858" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_69974853" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69974853" />
    <a title="Ferris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/819818-ferris"><img alt="Ferris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207863553p2/819818.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69974853?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ferris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ferris" href="/user/show/819818-ferris">Ferris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69974853" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8377575302281597732">I really enjoyed this novel. Dostoevsky's characters were engaging, if melodramatic. I didn't want to put the book down! What would happen to little Nellie? Would Alyosha pick Natasha or Katya? Would the prince get what was coming to him? You'll have to read it to find out. The major theme was identified in the title, "The Insulted and Humiliated". Dostoevsky points out the cruel behavior of the aristocracy towards the "common" person, and Dostoevsky favors the ability of the downtrodden to main</span>
  <span id="freeText8377575302281597732" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this novel. Dostoevsky's characters were engaging, if melodramatic. I didn't want to put the book down! What would happen to little Nellie? Would Alyosha pick Natasha or Katya? Would the prince get what was coming to him? You'll have to read it to find out. The major theme was identified in the title, "The Insulted and Humiliated". Dostoevsky points out the cruel behavior of the aristocracy towards the "common" person, and Dostoevsky favors the ability of the downtrodden to maintain their dignity by living by their principals, and relying on true love and loyalty. The issue of forgiveness is also addressed, and the author provides several examples of forgiveness and lack thereof, and the consequences of those choices. Wonderful read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8377575302281597732" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Paul Ataua" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33831018-paul-ataua"><img alt="Paul Ataua" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407668489p2/33831018.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2091005081?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846645273" href="/book/show/329875.The_Insulted_and_Injured">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2091005081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6722580840820617289">'The insulted and the Injured' was Dostoevsky's first novel and an acorn from which the oaks that are 'Crime and Punishment', and 'The Brothers Karamazov' grew. It isn't as deep nor bites as hard as the later novels, and, at times, has the feel of a soap opera, but it reads well and as an interesting if ambiguous ending. Worth reading for that reason alone.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1862217138" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2852069781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3400327591329237901">ENGLISH: A typical great novel by Dostoievski, with great characters, although some of them are a little simple. Aliosha, for instance, is completelly will-less, while his father is one of the evilest characters of literature.<br/><br/>Natasha provides a good example of what C.S.Lewis meant in "The Four Loves" when he said that Eros (erotic love) can become a God that demands complete submission whatever the cost. Lewis illustrated this with this quote by Denis de Rougemont: <i>When love begins to be a god, </i></span>
  <span id="freeText3400327591329237901" style="display:none">ENGLISH: A typical great novel by Dostoievski, with great characters, although some of them are a little simple. Aliosha, for instance, is completelly will-less, while his father is one of the evilest characters of literature.<br /><br />Natasha provides a good example of what C.S.Lewis meant in "The Four Loves" when he said that Eros (erotic love) can become a God that demands complete submission whatever the cost. Lewis illustrated this with this quote by Denis de Rougemont: <i>When love begins to be a god, it begins to be a demon.</i> Natasha gives up everything (her parents, her betrothed, her own honor and reputation) as an offering to her love for a worthless man. And she knows this very well.<br /><br />Nellie, on the other hand, is one of the more complex children in literature, while her fate is not too different from that of her namesake in Dickens's novel (The old curiosity shop), which perhaps Dostoievski had read, even though the character of the two children is almost opposite.<br /><br />ESPAÑOL: Una gran novela, típica de Dostoievsky, con estupendos personajes, aunque algunos son un poco sencillos. Aliosha, por ejemplo, no tiene voluntad, mientras que su padre es uno de los personajes más malvados de la literatura.<br /><br />Natacha es un buen ejemplo de lo que quiso decir C.S.Lewis en "Los cuatro amores" cuando dijo que Eros (el amor erótico) puede convertirse en un Dios que exige sumisión completa, cueste lo que cueste. Lewis lo ilustró con esta cita de Denis de Rougemont: <i>Cuando el amor empieza a ser un dios, se convierte en un demonio.</i> Natacha lo abandona todo (padres, prometido, su honor y su reputación) como sacrificio a su amor por un hombre que no lo merece. Y ella es plenamente consciente de ello.<br /><br />Nellie, por otro lado, es una de las niñas más complejas de la literatura. Aunque su destino no difiere mucho del de su homónima en la novela de Dickens (La tienda de antigüedades), que tal vez Dostoievsky habría leído, el carácter de estas dos niñas es casi opuesto.<br /></span>

<div id="review_49781228" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49781228" />
    <a title="Frankie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/853659-frankie"><img alt="Frankie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1322158484p2/853659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49781228?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Frankie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Frankie" href="/user/show/853659-frankie">Frankie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/6342678-the-insulted-and-injured">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/853659-frankie?shelf=russian">russian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49781228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8785335700075131520">This really should be ranked among Dostoevsky's top few novels, based on character type alone. The concept he expands most here is forgiveness. The character types, especially the villain Prince Valkovsky, are complete and believable and timeless.<br/><br/>The first scenes remind me of <i>White Nights</i>, dipping into (then abandoning for the remainder of the novel) a romantic intrigue with the narrator. His temporarily beloved becomes the central character, as her reputation is sullied by her fiancee's father.</span>
  <span id="freeText8785335700075131520" style="display:none">This really should be ranked among Dostoevsky's top few novels, based on character type alone. The concept he expands most here is forgiveness. The character types, especially the villain Prince Valkovsky, are complete and believable and timeless.<br /><br />The first scenes remind me of <i>White Nights</i>, dipping into (then abandoning for the remainder of the novel) a romantic intrigue with the narrator. His temporarily beloved becomes the central character, as her reputation is sullied by her fiancee's father. Afterward the narrator is made completely impartial, seeming only to stand for the noble ideal or "judge" type. By the second subplot, the orphan doubles the spurned lover role providing the lower-class parallel to the society insults of the other subplot. The fiancee's father becomes a key character, the only and most consistent of Dostoevsky's evil men, who never yields or expresses regret.<br /><br />The most interesting thing about this novel is the twist, which I won't divulge. Dostoevsky doesn't normally have recourse to plot twists or aberrations, especially not at the end.<br /><br />Also, if I have any complaint, it's that the narrator's writing career wind-up in the epilogue seems to be Dostoevsky's dig at publishers and critics, and lends nothing to the plot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8785335700075131520" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3579864792" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3579864792" />
    <a title="Saleh Muhammed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/91979266-saleh-muhammed"><img alt="Saleh Muhammed" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597184970p2/91979266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3579864792?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Saleh Muhammed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Saleh Muhammed" href="/user/show/91979266-saleh-muhammed">Saleh Muhammed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3579864792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5924691680439782397">This is my re-reading of "The Insulted and Injured" as part of my attempt to complete the complete work of Feydor Dostoyevsky. I had read the novel for a very long time, when I began reading Dostoevsky, and at that time I did not have the literary interest that makes me search about this novel, and I get enough for saying: It is a good novel.<br/>I definitely knew that studying such a novel would require preparation. I did not begin it until I had finished with Henry Trouba's book “Dostoevsky: His Li</span>
  <span id="freeText5924691680439782397" style="display:none">This is my re-reading of "The Insulted and Injured" as part of my attempt to complete the complete work of Feydor Dostoyevsky. I had read the novel for a very long time, when I began reading Dostoevsky, and at that time I did not have the literary interest that makes me search about this novel, and I get enough for saying: It is a good novel.<br />I definitely knew that studying such a novel would require preparation. I did not begin it until I had finished with Henry Trouba's book “Dostoevsky: His Life - His Works,” which gives a good biography of Dostoyevsky's life and the politics of the Russian Messenger Journal, which apparently played a major role in his method, Which should have been boring when it was detailed, but it was beautiful, expressive, full of emotion and humanity.<br />I also benefited greatly from the study of Nicholas Trail, the literary critic, of Raskolnikov's character from Dostoyevsky's novel Crime and Punishment as well, Nicholas 'view that Dostoevsky's character with epilepsy dominated his work, and whoever reads the crime and punishment will not ask me for clarification of Nicholas' opinion, Raskolnikov is a fickle hero Strange as it sounds, he has seizures in every event, but Dostoevsky does not describe these seizures as epilepsy more than what he describes as fever, fatigue and exhaustion, except that they always come in the form of epileptic freaks.<br /><br />As for this novel, it also applies to the concept of Nicholas, as the hero not only has seizures (and by the way, this time the protagonist himself has verbally confessed those seizures) but also lives the life of Dostoevsky completely and suffers from the same problems, I must mention here that Dostoevsky began This work (I mean the novel) with the beginning of his serious work after his release from prison, he had written before him the village of Stepanchikovo and its inhabitants, and it was really one of his worst works, and by his own saying, it was a vulgar comic whose only goal is to collect money after his release from prison, as well as return to the world of writing.<br /><br />Our novel carried a certain seriousness that made Dostoevsky himself a hero in it. The problem of the protagonist is very similar to the problem of Raskolnikov, as well as the problem of "Basement Man", a poor, proud writer looking for a way out to get rid of his misery and misery, and in fact I see that Dostoevsky in this novel was influenced by the Victorians most influenced Especially the later ones: the coincidences he put in the novel only occur in a Victorian / classic novel, such as Oliver Dickens or Hugo's Hunchback, and this is what made Russian critics (who were very real at the time) describe the novel as "extremely fictional."<br /><br />As I mentioned at the beginning, the policy of Russian magazines, especially the magazine "The Russian Messenger" and "Time" magazine has greatly influenced Dostoevsky's writings. The novel is long and not in excess. Dostoyevsky takes whole pages in some descriptions that could have been reduced to one or two words, In some absolutely insignificant dialogues he takes an entire chapter, yet (and this is what distinguishes Dostoyevsky from Tolstoy in lengthening) Dostoevsky's lengthening was a little more than a filler.<br /><br />Dostoevsky's epileptic personality is reflected in the main characters: Ilyosha, Ivan, and Helen, and the three are psychologically misfits due to their occasional seizures, and the main character, Ivan, features Dostoyevsky's defiant character at the time. Vissarion Belinsky, a great Russian critic at the time, after Fedor wrote his first book "The Poor," which was the most beautiful opening by a writer at the time, his subsequent writing came like a dream of Uncle and others until his imprisonment, and no one expected him to produce something of value, especially Belinsky.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5924691680439782397" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_6017081" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6017081" />
    <a title="Matthew Reed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61459-matthew-reed"><img alt="Matthew Reed" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181415882p2/61459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6017081?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Reed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Reed" href="/user/show/61459-matthew-reed">Matthew Reed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Doestoevsky fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6017081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14000356206659358829">This is an early work by Dostoevsky that you don't hear much about. He wrote it after Notes from the House of the Dead and before <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17876.Notes_From_Underground' title='Notes From Underground by Fyodor Dostoevsky' rel='nofollow noopener'>Notes from Underground</a>. Sometimes the title is translated "The Insulted and the Injured" but I am glad this one is translated "The Insulted and the Humiliated" because it sounds less like a soap opera. The titles comes from a memorable speech by one of the main characters:<br/><br/>"O Lord, I thank Thee for all, for all, for Thy wrath and for Thy mercy! ... And for Thy sun whic</span>
  <span id="freeText14000356206659358829" style="display:none">This is an early work by Dostoevsky that you don't hear much about. He wrote it after Notes from the House of the Dead and before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17876.Notes_From_Underground" title="Notes From Underground by Fyodor Dostoevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Notes from Underground</a>. Sometimes the title is translated "The Insulted and the Injured" but I am glad this one is translated "The Insulted and the Humiliated" because it sounds less like a soap opera. The titles comes from a memorable speech by one of the main characters:<br /><br />"O Lord, I thank Thee for all, for all, for Thy wrath and for Thy mercy! ... And for Thy sun which is shining upon us again after the storm! For all this minute I thank Thee! Oh, we may be insulted and humiliated, but we're together again, and let the proud and the haughty who have insulted and humiliated us triumph now! Let them throw stones at us!"<br /><br />If it weren't written by Dostoevsky I am not sure that it would have even been translated into English, but we should be thankful that it was written by him, because after all not all books can be masterpieces and there are probably tons of Russian novels that we simple English speakers will never have access to simply because they were not written by Tolsoy or Dostoevsky. But this was definitely written by Dostoevsky. It has everything you'd expect: epilepsy, tragic interconnected love triangles, alcoholism, idealistic radical intellectuals, murder, dreams, madness, brain fever, long passionate speeches in candlelit rooms in the dodgy parts of 19th century Petersburg. <br /><br />The book has it's problems. It is melodramatic at times. The evil characters are a bit too evil, the simple minded characters a bit too simple minded. That said, there it does have a lot going for it. Like many of his other books, it is told from the perspective of a narrator who is writing notes or a diary rather than a novel as such. Unlike most of his other novels, though, this is written by a hack writer. The neat part is the hack writer is not writing a novel (or is he?) but rather recollections with a view to explain his troubles. The result is a tension between a very literary style and also a wild and almost incoherent passion to just tell the story simply. Of all his novels, this has the most literary references that I can remember (that is not to say allusions; after all this is a book by a writer, and you know how writers are with dropping names). There are constant mentions of Pushkin, Gogol, and Schiller, and then a few other guys who I didn't recognize. <br /><br />I enjoyed the book, but I wish I'd picked a more soothing book to read this month. Some of it hit just a little close to home and I was on the verge of developing a brain fever myself.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14000356206659358829" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8722542100118741941">Easily became my new favourite.<br/><br/>This 'lost' work of his (due to either not highly renowned as his Crime and Punishment or Karamazov Brothers or mass availability) has a slightly different style.<br/><br/>His potrayal of betrayal, suffering in the early part is very real, that one might ask, "is this based on a true story?", as the backcover stated? <br/>Moving further through the pages, Dostoevsky later aoapt a different style, particularly in part 4, where the story is non-linear in time with recollections fro</span>
  <span id="freeText8722542100118741941" style="display:none">Easily became my new favourite.<br /><br />This 'lost' work of his (due to either not highly renowned as his Crime and Punishment or Karamazov Brothers or mass availability) has a slightly different style.<br /><br />His potrayal of betrayal, suffering in the early part is very real, that one might ask, "is this based on a true story?", as the backcover stated? <br />Moving further through the pages, Dostoevsky later aoapt a different style, particularly in part 4, where the story is non-linear in time with recollections from much earlier. I think this is due to his stress on the theme (humiliation and insult), where it sort of became the climax. <br /><br />Nevertheless, I admired the complex sisterhood bond between Natasha and Katya, the narrative intertwined with Nelly toward the ending, the slightly detective style where it keeps you guessing, and most importantly, the sense of thriller where you just want to keep on reading the next page just to know what is going to happen next. Sounds a bit weird—I know—for a writer best known for writing on the theme of suffering, agony and anguish (there's a considerable high amount of crying in this); but perhaps that's due to this is one of his early works and this work of his is not considerably known to his readers let alone the general public. <br /><br />Lastly, I found that he placed himself as the third person in most scene/dialogue, and the morals that he thinks or said in response to a situation or question is what, I myself, uttered or think most of the time. <br /><br />Maybe it is just me, but share it if you too have gone through the same experience. <br /><br />9/10</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12083521921984484952">A bit soap-opera-ish, with a touch of Dickens. Worth a read none-the-less.<br/><br/>The narrator, Vanya, is a novelist in the mold of the author. One of his critics says that his books border on mawkish, and are stained by Vanya's sweat and tears, who works with such febrile intensity to complete them (always under the pressure of a deadline). This weakness is apparent in Dostoevsky's characterizations as well, as nearly everyone in this novel is in a near constant state of delirious emotional upheaval, c</span>
  <span id="freeText12083521921984484952" style="display:none">A bit soap-opera-ish, with a touch of Dickens. Worth a read none-the-less.<br /><br />The narrator, Vanya, is a novelist in the mold of the author. One of his critics says that his books border on mawkish, and are stained by Vanya's sweat and tears, who works with such febrile intensity to complete them (always under the pressure of a deadline). This weakness is apparent in Dostoevsky's characterizations as well, as nearly everyone in this novel is in a near constant state of delirious emotional upheaval, convulsing (often literally) after every confrontation, as the narrator rushes from one scene to the next with no respite even in dreams. It is easy, not to mention disconcerting, to imagine Dostoevsky on the verge of a nervous attack, in hot-pursuit with his pen as he feels all the emotions as he describes them. It is impossible to sustain a climax for hundreds of pages, so this intensity undermines the arc of the story as the reader habituates to the style, and any revelations only have the force of added melodrama when they emerge.<br /><br />Each character is again an exaggerated "type," although they are more involving and believable than in Dostoevsky's prior work, and seem based in part on his experience of individuals he knew in reality. <br /><br />There are hints of Dostoevsky's full powers at work here, though obscured by over-use of certain techniques and under-maturation of his literary/philosophical themes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12083521921984484952" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer112379973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11533853448479355659">This was the first of Dostoevsky's full-length novels. With the characteristic Dostoevskian emphasis on suffering this melodramatic story features a young writer (not unlike Dostoevsky himself) who is in love young girl who, in turn, loves another young man named Aloysha. Ivan has philosophical disputations with Aloysha's father, Prince Valkovski. The melodrama is further heightened by the presence of another girl, Nelly, who is harassed by the Prince. The melodramatic sequences reminded me of e</span>
  <span id="freeText11533853448479355659" style="display:none">This was the first of Dostoevsky's full-length novels. With the characteristic Dostoevskian emphasis on suffering this melodramatic story features a young writer (not unlike Dostoevsky himself) who is in love young girl who, in turn, loves another young man named Aloysha. Ivan has philosophical disputations with Aloysha's father, Prince Valkovski. The melodrama is further heightened by the presence of another girl, Nelly, who is harassed by the Prince. The melodramatic sequences reminded me of early Dickens, but the novel is buoyed throughout by signposts of the greatness immanent in Dostoevsky's literary style.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11533853448479355659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
